Essays

Mirage

— Zhan Lu

A mirage was recently discovered off the coast of Qingdao. It was as tall as a two-story building and appeared twice, in the morning and afternoon, causing a sensation with crowds of onlookers. This is worth recording.

Records of mirages along the Shandong coast are countless in the writings of the past. Wang Ningzhai's "Autumn Clear Miscellany" has a passage recording the Dengzhou mirage, which seems even more spectacular than today's Qingdao mirage. I will excerpt it below...

"Mirages in my hometown are most common in Dengzhou's Penglai Pavilion. Usually around the spring and summer equinoxes, when the easterly wind comes slowly and the water is calm, people often see them. The appearance of the mirage varies. Sometimes city walls rise faintly, resembling a metropolis; sometimes countless tiles are arranged neatly, thousands of doors are opened wide, with carriages bustling and people milling like weaving; sometimes forests and pagodas appear, with towering pavilions and magnificent halls, dazzlingly beautiful. Sometimes peaks rise abruptly, with thousands of chapters of summer trees, exotic animals, and rare birds, like a painting. In the early years of the Shunzhi reign, the garrison commander of Dengzhou held a banquet for guests in the pavilion. As they were drinking and chatting merrily, suddenly countless battleships appeared, covering the sea, and the people on board were all soldiers in armor with spears. The garrison commander was alarmed, thinking they were pirates, and immediately called off the banquet and went on alert. Suddenly, the ships and people all disappeared, leaving only the sea and sky, shimmering together."

Originally, all things in the world are like mirages! The Qingdao mirage disappeared after one hour in the morning and after half an hour in the afternoon. One should view all things in the world in this way. When they disappear, what difference is there between reality and illusion? As for the battleships seen on Penglai Pavilion, after a blink of an eye, only the sky and sea remain! Wang Ningzhai's account is a refreshing remedy for those who pursue militarism and expansion.

Shaving

— Huo Xueming

Without historical research, everyone would know that the hair of the ancients was always tied up in a bun on top of the head, covered with crowns, scarves, hats, etc. Zengzi even said, "Our body, hair, and skin are received from our parents, and we dare not damage them." Therefore, apart from washing and combing, they probably did not shave their hair. Until Huang Shangu's collection of poems included the line "If the heart is at home without leaving home, why bother to seek a book of shaving?", it made people wonder: What was in that book, which is now lost? According to Li Diaoyuan, this book may have been similar to the "Eye Song" of the Tang Dynasty. The "Eye Song" is a short text for shaving a child's newborn hair, which may not be accurate. Therefore, it is not easy to clearly determine whether the ancients shaved their hair or not. But as I see it, they only trimmed their sideburns and shaved their beards at most. There are two reasons for this: (1) They did not want to become shaved monks because they wanted to be loyal to the emperor and filial to their parents. (2) They wanted to be Zhou Bian and Yin Xu, cultivating an elegant and noble demeanor to show the dignity of the special class of scholar-officials. Li Bai's letting down his hair and drifting on a boat, and Qu Yuan's disheveled hair while wandering, were just attempts to imitate the unceremonious fishermen and woodcutters in the great nature after being resentful of the world.

This trend of not shaving one's hair continued until the fall of the Ming Dynasty, when it disappeared. The Qing Dynasty ordered the entire country to follow the Manchurian custom of wearing a queue (braid) and to start shaving the head. This was a very strange change in Chinese history. I remember a Ming Dynasty Confucian official who had a satirical poem:

"Hearing that heads can be shaved, now they are shaved. All heads can be shaved, and heads cannot be made without shaving. Let them shave freely, the head is still my head; look at the shavers, people will also shave their heads!" The meaning of this poem is to say that although the Great Ming has fallen to you, you will also fall to others! Finally, after 268 years of the country, it was overthrown by Mr. Sun Yat-sen's efforts, and the Republic of China was established 22 years ago! Not only was the queue abolished, but heads are now shaved into the style of the army, almost like monks. Not only men but also young ladies and old ladies have cut off their black hair and shaped it into cloud-like hair styles.

The affairs of the world, just from the shaving of the head alone, have become infinitely changeable and unpredictable. What's more, now, in the occupied Northeast provinces, before September 18th, the Five-Colored Flag was once raised, followed by the Blue Sky, White Sun, and Red Earth flag. And now, the Blue Dragon Red Sun Flag is flying again, and the country name has been changed to Manchuria. The former Qing emperor Puyi, who ruined the country, was actually supported as the orthodox!

The Manchurian state, whipped on by Japan, is attacking us, while at the same time, the Red Army in Jiangxi is also threatening the Nanjing government: mutually intertwined, the national affairs are shattered. You shave my head, and I shave your head. Each uses the other's method to treat the other's person. If we do not eliminate all the evils with the spirit of revolution, and at the same time cater to the trend of the times and reform politics, then it is indeed because Heaven has no eyes if China does not perish! Those who advocate the idea that disasters can revitalize a nation, creating the loss of rights and humiliation with compromise, such people will never be able to shave other people's heads; they only deserve to have their heads shaved by others!

Long novel

Northeast Fourth Company Commander

— Zhang Henshui

Chapter 7: Severing Affection with a Few Words and Weeping Behind Closed Doors, Shouldering Responsibility and Discussing Business Over Wine

Company Commander Zhao took the wine cup in front of his father, followed by Tian Qing's wine, and said with a smile, "My father is very happy today, you three can offer him a cup. But you can only offer him one cup. If you want to offer him more, then you can only let me represent him." Tian Qing laughed and said, "How about this, I'll be a little presumptuous and have a few drinking bouts with the old man. Let Company Commander drink the wine instead. Everyone, what do you think of this idea?" As he said this, he rolled up his sleeves and winked at Yin and Guan. Can Ziqiang saw this and immediately understood what he meant, so he said, "Okay, having a few bouts of drinking can also liven things up." As he said this, he turned his head and said to Zhao Weng, "Dad, have a few bouts with them. They're bullying you, so have a few bouts with them." Tian Qing stood up, still not sitting down, and stretched out his hand, waving it at Zhao Weng and saying, "Old man, come on, come on." People who like to drink always agree with someone who proposes drinking, so he also stretched out his hand and waved it at Tian Qing. Then, he started shouting loudly, "Five, six," and so on. Zhao Weng was very good at drinking bouts. In ten bouts, Tian Qing lost seven bouts. So Yin Deren and Guan Yaowu said, as they moved forward to help, and Zhao Weng then began the righteous battle. This commotion greatly concealed Zhao Weng's pent-up complaints. After the dinner was over, Zhao Ziqiang said to Guan Yaowu, "Father, let's go to the front yard." His heart suddenly moved, wondering why he had such a suggestion. Could it be that he wanted me to be a matchmaker? He was afraid of attracting attention from others, so he said to Zhao Weng, "As soon as I came in, I was just eating and drinking. I haven't even visited my relatives in the front yard. Now, I should pay my respects. Otherwise, if they are now in poverty, they might say that I look down on them." As he said this, he stood up and walked towards the outer courtyard. This visit of his will reveal countless twists and turns. (84)

A Glimpse of Japan

— Zheng Yimei

This was told to me by a friend who has lived in Japan for many years. I will record it on the left like keeping accounts.

Cherry blossoms are the national flower of Japan. Every year during the cherry blossom season, men and women carry flasks and travel along the roads in endless streams, as if the whole country is in a frenzy. Besides cherry blossoms, they also love lotus flowers. The Shinobazu Pond in Ueno Park is filled with lotus flowers blooming beautifully, mostly crimson and white. When the autumn wind brings coolness, the lotus seed pods mature, but they never pick the lotus seeds for food, letting them wither and decay.

The cost of living is more than twice as high as in our country. For example, eggs cost one dime each, and rice costs more than forty yuan per dan. However, there are also some very cheap things, such as red bean cakes, which cost only two copper coins per piece. Like the wild chicken droppings eaten by the lower classes in our country, they are wonderful items to fill the stomach.

Most parks have fishing facilities. To enjoy this activity, you must buy a ticket. For the price of one dime, you can get a fishing rod. Clear water ripples, and you can fish at will. It is truly an elegant and interesting pursuit. However, these rods are baked over a fire and are very fragile. Greedy friends like to catch big fish, but the rod cannot bear the weight and breaks easily. Experienced people only catch small fish, catching one after another until the rod breaks, catching dozens of fish. The seller provides a large glass tank, so you can take the live fish home.

The Japanese are obsessed with cleanliness. Houses are thoroughly cleaned every week. Even in the summer, there are no crawling bedbugs in the walls. In their mouths, they don't call them bedbugs, but "Nanjing bugs." Could this be a specialty of Nanjing in our country? That would be too arbitrary.

The Japanese like to eat Chinese food very much. In Tokyo, there is the China Building, a purely Chinese restaurant. Most of our students flock to it, and the Japanese also admit that it is cheap and delicious, often inviting friends to feast there. Stir-fried intestines are fatty, delicious, and fragrant, especially to their taste. The intestines are pork intestines, because their chefs do not know how to cook pork intestines, so they can only go to the China Building to taste the unique flavor.

Soldiers must have a certain weight when they enlist. During conscription, some people often deliberately do not eat for several days, starving themselves until they are emaciated. This is similar to the "broken arm old man" sung by our ancient poets. War is dangerous and frightening, so it is no wonder the Japanese do this.

Cherry blossom girls bathing in the open air is not unusual. Sometimes, they swim and play in the waves, and when they are tired, they dig sand into a pit and bury their naked bodies in it, as if they are embracing a lifetime of quilts. According to them, it is very comfortable. The mystery of women has been completely broken, so hiring a model for sketching in that country is very cheap. For four yuan a week, you can make her sit naked all day long.

The Shiba Park prohibits Chinese and Koreans from entering, like the foreign Bund park in Shanghai before it was opened, which prohibited Chinese and dogs from entering. Their reason for the ban is that Chinese and Koreans spit everywhere, which easily spreads diseases and is a major obstacle to hygiene.

Wit and Humor

— Drunk Crazy Student

Praying for Peace: A certain Mr. Jia is known to be henpecked. One day, he angered his wife over a trivial matter and was forced to kneel in front of the bed. A friend happened to come and saw Mr. Jia groveling. Surprised, he asked, "What are you doing?" Mr. Jia hesitated and said, "You don't know my suffering. I am 'praying for peace'!" Luring the Enemy Deep: A certain woman, Mrs. Jia, accused a certain Mr. Yi of rape. The judge summoned both parties for questioning. Mr. Yi hired a lawyer to defend him, saying that Mrs. Jia did not resist at the time, so it cannot be called rape. The judge then asked Mrs. Jia, "If you say he raped you, why did you adopt a non-resistance policy?" Mrs. Jia replied, "I was not being non-resistant. I was 'luring the enemy deep'!"

A Paradise for Women

Housewife's Knowledge

— Chu

Child-Rearing Essentials

First Month After Birth丨The baby's weight at birth is not to be neglected. The eyes and umbilical cord must be kept clean. Sleep should be more than twenty hours. Do not make loud noises to startle the baby during sleep. Feeding times should be regular and not haphazard. If the baby cries after waking, give warm boiled water. It is best to breastfeed every three hours. Wash the breasts with boric acid solution or wipe them clean with medicated cotton wool. Do not hold the baby in your arms constantly. Bedding must be kept clean, and wet diapers should be changed immediately. The pillow should not be too high, and the baby's head should be moved frequently during sleep to prevent the head from becoming misshapen. The water for bathing should be between 29 and 40 degrees Celsius. Each bath should last about seven or eight minutes and not be too long. The best time for bathing is 10 a.m. or 2 p.m. Be sure that no soapy water remains on the skin after bathing.

Second Month After Birth丨If the baby cries outside of feeding times, give warm boiled water. Prevent the bad habit of sucking fingers in the mouth and prevent unclean hands from rubbing the eyes. Pay attention to the color of the stool. If the stool is blocked, dilute maltose with warm water and give two or three spoonfuls, or dip two or three paper twists into soapy water and insert them into the anus.

Third and Fourth Months After Birth丨On windless, sunny days, move the baby to the outdoors in a lightweight crib to receive sunlight, but pay attention to the season. In winter, do this at noon; in spring and autumn, between 10 a.m. and 3 p.m.; and in summer, before 10 a.m. and after 4 p.m. However, when sunbathing, the baby's face should not be directly in the sun. Early summer is the best time of year for sunbathing because the ultraviolet rays in the sunlight are twice as strong as in winter.

Fifth Month After Birth丨At this time, stop feeding at night. Gradually reduce the number of feedings each night to avoid indigestion.

Sixth Month After Birth丨Vaccinate at this time. If the baby shows signs of discomfort, first take the temperature and then consult a doctor. Do not listen blindly to others or engage in superstitious activities such as praying to gods and Buddhas.

Seventh and Eighth Months After Birth丨Prepare to wean the baby by feeding pureed vegetables, radish soup, etc., two or three spoonfuls a day, gradually increasing the number of times and amounts. After two weeks, add a little salt to the soup, and gradually give thin rice water and porridge.

Ninth Month After Birth丨Frequently give fruit juice or freshly squeezed sugarcane, tangerine, or pear juice, or add a few drops of cod liver oil to the fruit juice. You can also add a little lard, butter, or white sugar soy sauce to the thin porridge.

Tenth Month After Birth丨At this time, the baby's sense of taste becomes more sensitive, and the baby can distinguish between good and bad. If the baby refuses to eat something because it is not good, you should comply when the baby wants something else. However, the baby should try everything that does not harm hygiene to avoid developing picky eating habits later.

Eleventh and Twelfth Months After Birth丨Do not glare or scold the baby. You should raise the baby gently. If the baby has any bad habits, correct them gradually at this time. If the baby likes music, choose peaceful melodies to please the baby's heart.

Household Knowledge

Cleaning丨In middle-class and upper-class families, cleaning is usually the duty of servants. However, they have no knowledge of hygiene and are often perfunctory. The housewife should supervise them personally, paying particular attention to the corners of the room and places without sunlight. In the countryside, you can use lime water instead of disinfectant.

Glass Windows丨To wipe glass windows, first wipe the window frame clean with lukewarm water, then wipe the glass. If lukewarm water does not clean it, you can use a little alcohol or ammonia water.

Pots丨Whether copper, iron, or enameled, pots for cooking should be scrubbed with rice straw ash after cooking to prevent rust.

Porcelain丨When cups, bowls, spoons, etc., are stained, they can be boiled in alkaline solution to make them as white as new.

Soup Stains丨Generally, if iron bottles used for holding dishes have soup stains, add a little soda and boil them with water to the full capacity of the bottle to remove the stains.

Ink Stains丨If clothes are stained with ink, wash them with milk to remove the stains easily.

Rice Bins丨When buying rice, you must take out the old rice. Do not leave any old rice in the corners of the bin because it easily breeds insects and reduces the nutritional value.

Page 15

Free Talk

The Voice of the Nation

— Shiheng

In last month's "Free Talk," Mr. XX wrote an article on "The Bachelor's Lament" and "Tears on a Barren Mountain." It is said that these two ditties have been sung to foreigners as national anthems. The former is about the Great Qing Dynasty, and the latter is about the current Party-State. Both are mournful and wailing. Although foreigners may not understand the meaning or words, they will understand the pitiful tunes. I think this pitiful tune is precisely the voice of the Chinese nation.

China currently has no widely recognized national anthem, which seems inevitable. What kind of tune can concretely represent the path this aging nation is taking and the general decline in the lives of its people? If a great composer actually created a solemn and majestic national anthem, could that song truly represent this nation that has fallen beyond any further fall? The life of this nation inevitably leads it to emit sounds like "Tears on a Barren Mountain" and "The Bachelor's Lament." Isn't that precisely its national voice?

Although we have no national anthem, in recent years, there have been tunes that men, women, old, and young can all recite. The representative of these tunes is something like "Drizzling Rain." At first, it only circulated in the Shanghai area, but later it became popular throughout the country. Many primary schools even taught them to boys and girls. But aren't these popular tunes largely the same as "Tears on a Barren Mountain" and "The Bachelor's Lament"?

But why did these new mournful and wailing tunes become so rapidly popular throughout the country? Because they perfectly fit the life of this nation. A national life perfectly suited to this kind of leisurely and bored song. We wonder, what kind of leisurely and bored national life is that?

New art critics analyze the most popular "Jazz Music" in Europe and America, saying that it is music that expresses the victory of the bourgeoisie. So, what does our "Drizzling Rain" express? Couldn't we say that it expresses the sound of our nation's death?

Dark Night

— Yu Dafu

What, what? None of those things were written by me. What could I write? Lately, I've been very afraid, afraid of people mentioning me. The wind is really strong tonight. I'm afraid a few more boats will capsize in the river! Ah, ah, ha, what, the lights went out? Oh, it's back, ah, it dimmed again. Wait a moment, it'll probably come back. Sitting here in the darkness like this is kind of interesting. Oh, do you have matches? Wait a moment, let me find a candle. … Ouch, damn it, the chair broke my leg! It's okay, it's okay, good, I got it…

The candlelight is quite fun. Alas, do you remember? The article I wrote today imitating a primary school textbook... "In the third month of late spring, peonies are in full bloom. I and my friends are playing in the courtyard. Swallows fly in, diligently seeking food. Can we be less diligent than birds?" I just thought of another one, "Li Sheng studies diligently at night. The northwest wind rises and blows out the electric light, the light of the candle." Alas... I can't do anything lately, but I can still write these kinds of little articles, not bad, right? My wife? Sigh, I hope she's not sick! I'd like to go back for a visit during the summer vacation. But I'm afraid that going back for a visit will lead to another child, which would be too troublesome. Do you have any more wine there? Oh, don't blow out the candle! The wind is really strong! It's terrible! … Go get it, the wine? Wait a moment, grab a box of matches, I'll go with you. … The lights in the corridor are out too? Be careful of the stairs! Oh, it's out! Damn it, let's not light it, it'll just blow out anyway when we go outside. … Oh, oh, what a strong wind! Cold! So cold! … Sigh!

Beyond Two Types of People

— Li You

Mr. Bofu divides people into two types: those with a prose temperament and those with a poetic temperament. (See the May 17th Free Talk)

But I believe that beyond these two types of people, there also exists a dramatic temperament - and it is the most common. These people do not need to cater to the world, nor do they necessarily need to make the world suit them; they simply act freely and always find themselves in advantageous situations. They only look for opportunities. At one moment, they seem as lovable as people with a prose temperament; but at another moment, they appear as lofty as people with a poetic temperament.

Without these people with a dramatic temperament, the world would seem lonely and monotonous! Because they are the movers of all tragedies and the manipulators of all comedies.

Historically, the great achievements of these people are recorded the most. In prosperous times, they create rituals, compose music, sing praises, fawn over superiors, slander peers, betray friends, and think of ways to please their masters. In chaotic times, they support A and denounce B, then support C and denounce A; "good birds choose trees to perch on," so they may as well support B and denounce A or C again. Especially during dynastic changes, they are the ones who curse the rebels, punish and dismantle those who died for the country, surrender to the enemy, praise the holy ruler, and suddenly switch sides. "He who kills my lord is my enemy, and he who kills my enemy is my lord." With just a turn of the tongue, they can become veterans of five dynasties.

People with a rich dramatic temperament are the most adaptable. You see them clearly in that circle, but actually they are not; when you look again, they are already outside this circle. Yesterday we were good friends, today we can call the police to arrest you when we meet on the road. If the revolution succeeds tomorrow, I will turn around and be your old comrade again.

The most important characteristic of people with a dramatic temperament is that they have a well-trained tail, which wags when it should wag, stands erect when it should stand erect, and tucks in when it should tuck in, truly without anything that is not beneficial. The French poet La Rochefoucauld said: "People are like dogs, they also have long tails and short tails. Some people's short tails are innate, some are because they did not do well. People with short tails are in a difficult position… but people with long tails are happy." Today is an era of encouraging "confession" and "denunciation," quickly train yourself to grow a long tail! If unfortunately your tail is naturally short.

Therefore, people with a prose temperament are often successful. (Conversely, those who die violently are the "king who succeeds and the bandit who fails" has been the case since ancient times) People with a poetic temperament are often parasites. (Mr. Bofu's words) Only people with a dramatic temperament are eternally victorious!

Ramblings from Qu Lu

— Zizhan

(35) Talking About Ghosts

Spring arrives in the village on the Qingfeng River,

My hometown should have souls unsummoned.

As a guest, I shamelessly talk about ghosts,

Unknowingly, the world turns gloomy and dark.

This is one of my ten untitled quatrains from six years ago in spring. At that time, I didn't talk about national affairs or read the news. When friends met, they only talked about foxes and ghosts to amuse themselves. This poem is a summary of this kind of life and its mood. My late father was well-versed in Strange Tales from a Chinese Studio and often praised Pu Liuxian's character. My fondness for talking about foxes and ghosts also stemmed from childhood. I still remember a quatrain I wrote on a painting of Pu Liuxian writing his book:

Let me speak wildly, let them listen wildly,

Rain like silk falls on bean trellises and melon vines.

I suppose they are tired of human talk,

And love to hear ghosts sing poems in autumn graves.

My late father said that Pu Liuxian had anti-Manchu ideas. All the foxes he mentioned were secretly denouncing the Manchus: "fox" sounds like "Hu," referring to the northern barbarians. This book was initially titled Fox Ghost Tales, but fearing the taboo of the time, he changed it to its current name. However, although the book never explicitly criticized the authorities, it actually viewed them as foxes and goblins. At that time, scholars did not distort their learning or fawn on the world to gain profit and rank. Even those who immersed themselves in ancient books and researched the classics often faced great lawsuits if their words were careless. With the government treating scholars so cruelly, talking about foxes and ghosts was also a way for scholars to avoid disaster. It is said that Su Dongpo offended the authorities with his writing and was exiled to Lingnan. He dared not approach pen and ink again and only forced himself to tell ghost stories to pass the long days. When people contradicted him, he would say, "Let me speak wildly." Telling ghost stories to dispel evil was also a good intention of scholars.

(36) A Supplement to "Confucius and Drama"

The book Confucius and Drama collects details quite thoroughly. Here are two more pieces of information that can be added. One is the story of Kong Zonghan of the Song Dynasty. When his father, Daofu, was an envoy to the Khitans, he refused to allow actors to perform a play using Confucius as a subject. He also advised Emperor Zhezong that it was inappropriate to allow actors in the imperial theater to perform plays about Confucius. It can be said that like father, like son. Wang Pi of the Song Dynasty wrote in his Mianshui Yantan Lu: "During the Yuanyou period, on the Lantern Festival, the emperor visited Yingxiang Pool to feast with his ministers. The imperial theater's actors performed a play using the former sage as a subject. Kong Zonghan, a vice minister of the Ministry of Justice, submitted a memorial, saying that during the reign of Emperor Wenzong of the Tang Dynasty, there had been a similar play, and the emperor ordered it to be dismissed. Now, should the holy emperor still tolerate this when he is feasting with his ministers? The emperor ordered the censors to deal with it. Someone said, 'This is a minor matter, why bother mentioning it?' Kong replied, 'You don't understand. The emperor is in his prime, and he should respect virtue and enjoy the Dao. But the base actors are so disrespectful. If this is tolerated and not corrected, will it not harm the emperor's virtue?' Those who heard this were ashamed and impressed." Another incident occurred in the ninth year of the Yongle reign of the Ming Dynasty, when Emperor Chengzu prohibited lyrics and songs that insulted sages and worthies. Confucius was certainly among those who could not be insulted. From this, it can be said that from the early days of Tang and Song drama, it was not permitted to use Confucius as a subject. During the early Ming Dynasty, when opera flourished, the performance of plays featuring sages and worthies was strictly prohibited. No wonder the performance of the play "Zi Jian Nanzi" at Qufu Normal School caused a great uproar. Since Confucius has been honored and praised by emperors for two thousand years since Emperor Wu of the Han Dynasty, how could the Kong clan tolerate joking with Confucius in the land of his birthplace? This is the origin of the lawsuit over insulting Confucius.

Red Carrot Whiskers Lice (Continued)

— Jules Renard, translated by Lin Qu

Mr. Lepic’s… Really, Red Carrot Whiskers, you really make us not understand. You've reached such an age and have grown into a child as big as you are, you should be ashamed. I won’t even talk about your feet being so dirty, maybe you just saw it when you came home. But the lice are biting you, and you neither ask your teachers to clean you up, nor do you ask the people at home to get rid of them. Please tell us, what exactly is the pleasure of letting them eat you alive like this. There is still some blood left in your messy hair.

Red Carrot Whiskers… This is because the comb scratches me uncomfortably.

Mr. Lepic… Ah! This is because the comb scratches you uncomfortably. Well then, is this how you repay your sister? Do you hear what he says, Elerstin? This delicate gentleman is not satisfied with his barber. My daughter, I advise you to immediately let this voluntary martyr be eaten by his parasites.

Elerstin… I’m just going to let it go today, mom. I only shaved off the most crowded spots for him, and I’ll have to comb him a second time tomorrow. But I know a girl who has lice on her head but still uses perfume.

Mr. Lepic… Never mind about others. As for you, Red Carrot Whiskers, give me that basin and put it on the low wall in the garden. This should call the whole village to come and see it so you can be ashamed.

Red Carrot Whiskers takes the basin out; he puts the basin under the sun, and he guards it beside it.

The first to come closer to look was old Granny Nanette. Every time she meets Red Carrot Whiskers, she stops, observing him with her near-sighted and cunning little eyes, moving the black soft hat on her head as if she had guessed something.

"What is this thing?" she said.

Red Carrot Whiskers did not answer anything. She bent down to look at the basin.

"Are these shelled small lentils? Really, I simply can't see clearly. My son Pierre really should have gotten me a pair of glasses long ago."

She touched it with her fingertips, as if preparing to put it in her mouth to taste it. Judging from the situation, she certainly did not know what was in it.

"And what are you standing here doing? You seem angry, staring blankly. I can say with certainty that someone has scolded you, punished you to stand here. Listen to me, although I am not your grandmother, I am thinking about some things. Poor child, I sympathize with you because I know they must be making your life very difficult."

Red Carrot Whiskers glanced around, looking to see if his mother was nearby, and then said to old Granny Nanette…

"Then what should I do? Why does this matter concern you? You should just mind your own business and let me be quiet."

Like Brutus

Mr. Lepic… Red Carrot Whiskers, you didn't study as hard as I expected last year. Judging from your school report card, you really could have done a little better. But you're just thinking and reading books that are not allowed to be read at school. Your memory is originally very good, and your recitation scores are not bad, but you are unwilling to work hard on your exercises. Red Carrot Whiskers, you should become more serious in the future.

Red Carrot Whiskers… Please rest assured, Dad. True, I did slack off a bit last year. This time, I feel that I already have the ambition to work hard desperately. I don't boast that my total score can be the first in the whole class.

Mr. Lepic… But you have to try.

Red Carrot Whiskers… No. Dad, then you're asking too much. I'm not good at geography, German, physics, chemistry, etc. There are only two or three people who get the best grades in these subjects, and they specialize in these subjects. It's impossible to catch up with them; but I think – listen to me, Dad – I think I can get first place in the Chinese language subject soon, and I won't let anyone take it away again in the future. Even if I work hard desperately and still can't get first place, at least I can have a clear conscience. I can proudly write like Cicero: Ah, "Virtue"! You are just a noun.

Mr. Lepic… Ah! My child, I believe you will use these words.

Felix… What did he say, Dad?

Elerstin… I didn't hear clearly either.

Mrs. Lepic… Me too. Say it again, Red Carrot Whiskers.

Red Carrot Whiskers… Ah! I didn't say anything, Mom. (To be continued)

Page 16

Economic Supplement, Issue 24, Published Every Monday

Taxing Imported Rice and Achieving Self-Sufficiency in Food Xu Shuji

Since last year, the fall in rice prices has been truly unexpected. To speculate on the reasons for this decline, they can be roughly divided into two categories: (1) External reasons, namely (a) the global economic panic, and (b) the surge in imported rice; (2) Internal reasons, namely (a) the lack of political clarity, (b) the economic recession, (c) social instability, and (d) the numerous inherent problems in agriculture itself. What are the consequences of the fall in rice prices? The agricultural economy is in a difficult situation, without the strength to improve the land, and productivity naturally declines. Since the foundation of China's national economy is still built on agriculture, if agriculture is in this state, the entire national economy will inevitably be affected. Now, to revitalize agriculture and forestry, how should we maintain the price of grain, the only product of farmers? However, maintaining grain prices is a complex issue that cannot be fully covered in this short article. Let us first discuss the issue of rice tariffs.

The issue of grain tariffs has been debated for decades in European countries and Japan and is now a thing of the past. In China, however, it is a fresh issue. To discuss this issue, we should first understand the trend of rice imports over the years and list the statistical figures from the customs trade records since the founding of the Republic (unit: dan):

Year Quantity Year Quantity

Year 1 2,700,391 Year 11 19,156,182

Year 2 5,414,896 Year 12 22,434,962

Year 3 6,814,003 Year 13 13,198,054

Year 4 8,476,058 Year 14 12,624,624

Year 5 11,284,023 Year 15 18,700,797

Year 6 9,837,182 Year 16 21,091,581

Year 7 6,984,025 Year 17 12,616,154

Year 8 1,809,749 Year 18 10,822,805

Year 9 1,151,752 Year 19 19,892,784

Year 10 10,629,245 Year 20 10,740,810

Although the imported rice listed in this table does not increase regularly year by year, the general trend of increase is evident. Looking back at the Tongzhi and Guangxu eras of the late Qing Dynasty, the difference between then and now is even more striking! Even during the Tongzhi and Guangxu years, the annual rice imports only exceeded 10 million dan in Guangxu 21st year (1895) and 33rd year (1907). There were only four years that exceeded 9 million dan, and in other years, the imports were at most just over 7 million dan, and as few as just over 6,000 dan (see Mr. Feng Liutang's article "A Brief Overview of Rice Imports Over the Past Two Hundred Years" in "The Shanghai Food Problem"). Comparing this with the recent years, we can see that the increase in rice imports has been considerable. What is the reason for this?

Historically, our country has not only failed to prevent the import of rice but has also sought to encourage it. Looking at the historical facts, we can understand this. For example, during the Yongzheng era of the Qing Dynasty, when Siamese rice arrived in Guangdong, Emperor Shizong of Qing ordered that it be sold at the current market price in Guangdong Province, and all goods on board were exempt from taxes. In the 21st year of Qianlong, it was also decided that Guangdong and Fujian merchants who transported rice should be rewarded. If merchants from Guangdong and Fujian prepared their own capital and obtained permits to go to countries such as Annam and Siam to transport rice back to the province, they would be granted official titles or caps according to the amount of rice transported. This shows how welcome the import of rice was. After the founding of the Republic, the import of grains such as rice, wheat, and corn was still tax-free. On the other hand, the export of grains was absolutely prohibited. Since the 39th year of Qianlong (1774), when six articles of the Grain Prevention Order were promulgated, those who transported rice out of the country were subject to extreme penalties. Even to this day, although the ban is no longer so strict, rice still cannot be exported. In comparison, we can see that China's rice policy throughout the ages has been to welcome the import of rice on the one hand and prevent the export of domestic rice on the other. The underlying reason is nothing more than a respect for the people's food supply, which is not inherently wrong. However, although the import of rice is sometimes necessary, allowing its free import at any time and in any place will inevitably lead to the danger of it overwhelming domestic rice. The export of rice should also be prohibited at times, but prohibiting all exports regardless of whether it is a year of abundance or scarcity is not a way to promote increased production. Therefore, China's rice policy, which has been passed down through the ages, is indeed a major mistake.

To discuss whether rice tariffs should be levied, there are several preconditions that should be discussed beforehand, namely:

(A) The question of whether domestic rice should be protected. Rice is the main grain in our country, which everyone knows. All countries in the world, except for a few industrial and commercial countries, protect their main grains. These grains are subject to tariffs when imported from abroad. The weight of the tariffs varies from country to country, but they all have a protective effect. Especially in recent years, with the "agricultural panic" spreading around the world, grain tariffs have been increasing year by year. We can see from two tables (tables omitted) in the "Agricultural Situation in 1930 to 1931" compiled by the International Agricultural Association: (1) The wheat import tax in countries such as Germany, France, and Italy in 1930 increased to more than double or twice that of 1913. (2) The grain import tax in Germany increased greatly in 1931 compared to 1930. Compared with the grain prices in London and Liverpool, the amount of import tax for various grains, except for corn, generally exceeds the corresponding grain prices, which shows that Germany's grain tariffs are very high. The same is true for other countries that adopt agricultural protection tariffs. This is not an unconscious increase in tariffs, because since 1929, grain prices in various countries around the world have fallen sharply, and they have a large backlog of goods. Various countries intend to adopt a "beggar-thy-neighbor" policy and strive to dump goods abroad. If country A does not raise tariffs, the grains of country B or country C will take advantage of the opportunity, and the grains of country A will inevitably be overwhelmed. Therefore, it is necessary to prevent this in advance. Among the countries in the world, except for a few countries or colonies, all have rice import taxes (see Mr. Wu Juenong's article "The Issue of Food Import and Tax Exemption" in "The Shanghai Food Problem" compiled by the Shanghai Municipal Social Bureau). For example, countries such as India, Siam, Taiwan, and Korea, which produce more rice and have surplus grain for export, are not afraid of competition from foreign rice. However, these countries have considerable import taxes. As for Japan, it is originally insufficient to be self-sufficient and seems to welcome foreign imports. However, in order to prevent foreign intrusion and encourage the production of domestic rice, it also has import taxes. Looking back at our country, last year (Year 21), after the autumn harvest in various provinces, the price of rice fell sharply, which was indeed due to a relatively good harvest. However, the main reason is not here. One reason is that the rice merchants in various places believed that there would be a major flood in the Yangtze River basin the year before (Year 20), and the harvest would definitely be poor, and rice prices would inevitably skyrocket. Therefore, they ordered a large number of forward rice from places such as Annam, Siam, and India. Unexpectedly, the opposite happened. The stockpiled rice could not be sold as much as possible, and the imported rice still kept coming. When the new grain came to market, farmers were forced by the economy to sell it one after another, and rice prices fell sharply. Furthermore, although domestic rice had a relatively good harvest, the rice exporting countries in the world have also had "bumper years" in recent years. Looking at the table excerpted below from the "Agricultural Situation in 1930 to 1931", we can understand this.

Asian Rice Exporting Countries (India, Siam, Taiwan, Korea, and Annam) Paddy Production (unit: million Metric Tons)

1927/28 59.7 1928/29 63.6

1929/30 62.2 1930/31 65.0

Originally, the international rice market was supplied by rice from India, Siam, Taiwan, Korea, and Annam, which accounted for almost nine-tenths of the total volume. Since the production of rice in these five places has greatly increased, its export volume has also increased accordingly. However, on the other hand, rice importing countries in Asia have also been affected by the global economic panic, and the market for important products has been very depressed (such as rubber in Ceylon and the Malay Peninsula, and rubber candy and coffee in the Dutch East Indies). As a result, purchasing power has been greatly reduced. Therefore, in foreign countries, the fall in rice prices is even more pronounced. From the rice price index table (1926 equals 100, table omitted due to limited space) published in the "Agricultural Situation in 1930 to 1931", it can be seen that foreign rice prices have fallen by almost half, or even more than half. There are no complete statistics on the production volume and prices of foreign rice between 1931 and 1932, so I dare not make a definite statement. However, in the 21st year of the Republic of China, rice imports reached 22,486,639 dan, an increase of 1.85.02 percent compared to the import volume in the 20th year. It also accounted for 11.36 percent of the net import volume in that year, setting the highest record since China imported rice. (See the third and fourth quarter trade reports of the International Trade Bureau.) It can be seen that domestic rice prices have fallen sharply, but foreign rice prices are also low, and foreign rice reserves are relatively large, so they have to be dumped in our country. Therefore, now, if we think that domestic rice prices have fallen sharply and foreign rice is no longer a concern, it is also incorrect. Therefore, the establishment of rice tariffs is even more necessary.

(B) The question of whether domestic rice is sufficient for self-sufficiency. If our country levies a protective import tax on rice, rice imports will certainly not be as easy as before. In case the amount of rice produced in various provinces is far from enough for consumption, we still have to rely on foreign rice. At this time, if we want to exempt import taxes, foreign rice will take the opportunity of rising domestic rice prices to flow in continuously, and domestic rice production will be greatly hit. If we want to levy the protective tax as usual, our people will have to eat foreign rice, and foreign rice will still be imported. Although the tariff can enter the national treasury, the tariff collected will eventually be passed on to domestic consumers. This is harmless to foreign rice producers and distributors, but it increases the burden on consumers, and the gains may not be worth the losses. Therefore, to levy an import tax on foreign rice, is domestic rice sufficient for self-sufficiency? Or if it is not sufficient, how much is lacking? This question also needs to be studied. According to the investigation, the import volume of rice in our country has increased rapidly in the past ten years, with at least 10 million dan or nearly 20 million dan per year. Including last year, there have been three times that the import volume has reached more than 20 million dan. Estimating its value, the leakage is truly alarming! However, after careful examination, I feel that although the import volume of rice is alarming, in fact, these quantities are still very small in the production or consumption volume of rice in our country. Therefore, I dare to boldly say that domestic rice is almost self-sufficient, and even if it is not sufficient, the amount is not large. To clearly explain this question, we must first know (1) the current production volume and future increase in production of rice, (2) the current consumption volume and future increase in consumption of rice, (3) the current cultivated area and future increase in cultivated area of rice, (4) the current total population and the rate of increase. However, there are no such statistics now, so how dare we talk about it? However, it may be possible to estimate it roughly. Let me briefly explain as follows:

Assuming that the amount of rice lacking in our country each year is exactly the same as the amount of rice imported, then the amount of rice imported each year since the import of rice should be regular, and even if it is due to population growth or increased demand, it should be gradual year by year. However, according to the table listed above, summarizing the import of rice in the past 20 years since the founding of the Republic, it is found that it fluctuates greatly and is rarely orderly. For example, it was 2.7 million dan in the first year of the Republic, and suddenly jumped to more than 5.41 million dan in the second year, increasing by more than double. In the eighth and ninth years, there were only more than 1 million dan, but in the tenth year, it suddenly jumped to more than 10 million dan, and since then it has continued to increase. In the twelfth year, it even reached more than 22 million dan, but in the thirteenth year, it suddenly dropped to more than 13 million dan. In the sixteenth year, it suddenly rose to more than 21 million dan. In the seventeenth and eighteenth years, it fell sharply again. In the nineteenth year, it almost reached 20 million dan. Such unstable fluctuations clearly have other special reasons mixed in, and it is definitely not that we need more than 10 million dan or 20 million dan of rice imports every year in ordinary years. What is the special reason? Because since the founding of the Republic, insect plagues, droughts, floods, or wars have occurred every few years, or continuously and mutually, resulting in famines in many provinces and a shortage of rice. Although some provinces may still have a surplus of rice, because the provinces are self-governing, they prohibit the export of rice. Even if there is a way to beg for grain, due to heavy taxes and many traffic obstacles, the rice cannot arrive as scheduled. Even if it can be transported, the amount is not much, and the price is particularly high. Isn't it more direct and convenient for the rice-deficient provinces to beg for rice from foreign countries? For example, the import of rice in Shanghai was particularly high in the 19th year of the Republic, because of the famine in the provinces of Jiangsu and Zhejiang in the 18th year. Also, the reason why rice imports exceeded 22 million dan last year was also because of concerns about the impact of the flood of the previous year. Even in ordinary years, although rice is still imported, it is also wrong to regard the import volume as the amount of rice lacking in our country. Why? As mentioned earlier, rice in our country and provinces is not freely circulated. Although some provinces have a surplus of rice, they cannot make up for the shortage in other provinces. For example, the rice in Fujian is certainly insufficient, but the people in these two provinces do not eat the rice from Hunan and Hubei, but prefer to eat rice from Annam and Siam, which is a clear proof. Therefore, it is also too crude to simply use the import of rice to prove to what extent the lack of rice in our country has reached. Now, let us go a step further and examine what percentage of the import volume of rice accounts for the production volume and consumption volume of rice in our country, which is the most important thing to determine whether rice is sufficient for self-sufficiency. According to the production volume of rice in our country, although there are many estimates from Chinese and foreign sources, the figures from various parties are vastly different and cannot be trusted. Therefore, it is also futile to examine what percentage of the import volume of rice accounts for the production volume. Now, let us put aside the production volume and export volume of rice and simply compare the import volume and consumption volume to know how many days of food the annual import volume of rice can provide, and we can also roughly estimate how much rice is lacking. According to Mr. Feng Liutang's example, "Fujian and Guangdong originally lacked rice and relied on foreign rice for supply. As for other provinces, unless it is an extremely famine year, the demand for foreign rice is not much. Let us take the year with the largest import of foreign rice in the country (the 12th year of the Republic) as an example. Converted into shi, it is only 14,956,641 shi. If we calculate that each person eats half a sheng per day, according to the national population of 400 million, it is about more than seven days of grain." (See page 96 of "The Shanghai Food Problem" compiled by the Shanghai Municipal Social Bureau.) The principle of his calculation is very reasonable. However, the import volume of rice in the past 20 years since the founding of the Republic varies from year to year. It is only an example to calculate the year with the most or least imports, but it is not enough for calculation. Now, according to his calculation principle, we will modify the calculation method. First, we will find the annual average of the import volume of rice from the first year to the 20th year of the Republic, which can be 11,321,508.5 dan (100 jin per dan). Converting it into shi (150 jin per shi), we can get 7,547,673 shi. It is certainly difficult to get an accurate number for the population statistics of our country. Let us take the recent survey results of the Ministry of the Interior as the standard. The total population of the country is 474,787,386 people. Assuming that the relevant number of people who eat rice in the entire population is 237,393,693 people (although it is clear how many people eat rice in the entire population, people in provinces such as Jiangsu, Anhui, Jiangxi, Fujian, Yue, Hunan, Hubei, Sichuan, Guangdong, Guangxi, Guizhou, and Yunnan generally eat rice. According to the population statistics of the Ministry of the Interior, the population of these provinces is more than 239 million, which already accounts for more than half of the total population. Although some people in these provinces do not eat rice, and some people outside these provinces also eat rice, they seem to be able to offset each other, so assuming that half of the population in our country eats rice, I think there is not much difference). According to common estimates, each person's daily rice consumption is five ge, then one day requires 1,186,968,465 ge of rice. Dividing this number by 7,547,673 shi, we can know that the average annual import volume in the past 20 years only provides 6.3 days of rice for the entire country. In other words, assuming that rice is never imported, our country only lacks 6.3 days of rice. Calculated by the number of days in the whole year, these 6.3 days only account for 1.72% of the number of days in the whole year. In other words, the import volume of rice only accounts for 1.72% of the annual consumption volume. Judging from this, we can see that the import volume of rice is very small in the consumption volume. If we think that the average annual import volume of rice since the 11th year of the Republic is far more than the average volume in the previous ten years, and it seems too small to use the average volume in the 2nd year, then let us find the average annual volume in the past ten years (11th year to 20th year), which can be 16,132,884.8 dan. Calculated by the same method as mentioned above, we can know that the average annual import volume only provides 9 days of rice for the whole country, that is, the import volume of rice only accounts for 2.4% of the annual consumption volume. Although this quantity is more than the previous calculation, it is not large. Mr. Hou Houpei also said, according to his calculation results, "Foreign rice imports account for about 4.3% of my country's rice consumption." (See page 14 of the "Food Import Issues" in the fourth issue of the second volume of the "International Trade Guide") Comparing this number with my calculation number, although it is almost doubled, it can also be seen that the import volume of rice is very small in the consumption volume. Judging from these facts, assuming that there are no droughts or floods in various provinces of our country, no bandits, no foreign insults, and farmers can live and work in peace and cultivate their fields diligently, even if rice is absolutely freely circulated between provinces, mutually adjusting each other, needless to say, farmers can improve rice fields and greatly increase rice production. Even if we operate well according to the indigenous method, I dare to boldly say that the rice produced annually in our country is almost enough to supply the consumption of the whole country. Our country does not eat foreign rice, nor will it starve to death; because the proportion of the import volume of rice to the national consumption volume is so small, and the rice consumed annually in the country for brewing and other purposes, although there are no definite statistics, the number is also very large. If our country all embraces the determination to endure hardship and adopt a policy of frugality, even if there is a slight shortage, it can be replaced by miscellaneous grains, so why rely on foreign rice every year? Moreover, the rice fields in our country have great potential for increasing yields. More than ten years ago, when I lectured at the Agricultural University of Beiping on "The Future of Agricultural Production in China," I estimated the increase in yields in the rice fields of nine provinces, including Jiangsu, Anhui, Jiangxi, Fujian, Yue, Hunan, Hubei, Sichuan, and Guangdong. If each mu can increase the yield of rice by one dou and six ge, the total amount of increased rice in the nine provinces can be more than 19,920,147 shi. The cultivated area used in this calculation is based on the household regulations of the former Qing Ministry of Revenue, and the number obtained is originally too small. However, it is already so large, and we can know that if these nine provinces or other provinces build extensive water conservancy and add paddy fields, the increase in rice production will far exceed 20 million shi. Then, not only will we not have to eat foreign rice, but we can also export the surplus to foreign countries. Therefore, once the rice tariff is levied, even if the import of rice is reduced or not imported at all, it will certainly not hinder the people who eat rice. This is another great reason why the rice tariff should be implemented.

(C) The question of whether rice prices should be increased. Some people think that once rice tariffs are levied, I am afraid that domestic rice prices will gradually increase, and consumers will suffer losses. The people who benefit may not be farmers, and the small farmers who buy rice to make a living between spring and summer may suffer even more. This certainly has a considerable reason. However, although there are no precise statistics on the proportion of the production cost and price of rice in various provinces in our country, the income and expenditure are generally just enough to offset each other, and there are even losses. As for the sharp drop in rice prices this year, farmers are losing money, which goes without saying. Assuming that rice prices continue to fall for a long time, who would be willing to plant rice again next year? Therefore, rice prices should be raised appropriately. However, once rice prices increase, foreign rice will take the opportunity to enter. Therefore, on the one hand, we must find ways to increase rice prices, and on the other hand, we must levy a small tariff to prevent the increase in rice imports. Moreover, comparing the prices of agricultural products with the prices of other commodities, the prices of agricultural products fall faster and more; conversely, when they rise, they are slower and smaller. This phenomenon exists in European and American countries, and it is also the case in our country. It can be understood by checking various statistics. In this regard, rice prices should also be raised a little. Therefore, it is only natural that rice prices will increase after the tariff is levied. If it is said that farmers may not benefit from the increase in rice prices after the tariff is levied, this is due to the failure of the food management law to be properly implemented, not the problem of the tariff system itself (the advantages and disadvantages of this are quite complex and will be discussed in detail in the future). What should be paid special attention to is that although the percentage of the import volume of rice to the national consumption volume is very small as mentioned above, the import of rice since the founding of the Republic has greatly increased compared to the Tongzhi and Guangxu years. In recent years, there has been a tendency to increase even more. If we do not levy tariffs and allow free imports, if there is another bumper harvest this year, I do not know to what extent rice prices will fall, and we will follow in the footsteps of British wheat and Japanese cotton. This is another reason why the implementation of rice tariffs should not be delayed.

From the above items (A), (B), and (C), there is no doubt that our country's rice

tariffs should be levied. Even the National Food Conference convened by the central government last year passed the principle of levying tariffs on grains. However, the new tariff implemented on May 22 this year still exempts rice, wheat, and wheat from import taxes, except for flour, which is subject to a tax of 25 cents per dan in gold units. What does this mean? Is it because Guangdong and Fujian need foreign rice to supply and not levy taxes? As mentioned in the various items in this article, even if the adjustment is proper, there is no fear of not having food to eat. Therefore, my proposition is that import of rice and grains should be taxed, but first, we should conduct a detailed survey of the price difference between foreign rice and domestic rice over the years, and investigate the production cost of rice and the living expenses of consumers, formulate standard prices, and compare them with the recent prices of foreign rice, and set the import tax rate according to the principle of protective tariffs. On the one hand, rice and grains in various provinces should be absolutely freely circulated to adjust surpluses and deficiencies, so that no province will suddenly run out of rice and have to rely on foreign rice. It is also necessary to find ways to increase rice production as soon as possible and strive for self-sufficiency. If we do this, the protective tariff on rice can be maintained permanently. If not, I am afraid that this tariff will have to be withdrawn once there is a rice shortage. This is the most important thing to pay attention to! Regarding this issue, I had proposed it twenty years ago. I am repeating old things, and the rice tax is still pending in the future.

This article, with over ten thousand words, elaborates in detail on the causes and consequences of the fall in rice prices and the issue of rice taxation, and is truly a masterpiece. However, due to the limited space of this publication, it cannot be fully published. There may be omissions that affect the meaning of the original work, so please pay attention to the reader!

Changes in Rice Prices Over the Last Five Years

— Editor: Zhou Wenjie

Grain is the main agricultural product. Farmers rely on it for national taxes, local levies, and their livelihoods, while the public depends on it for sustenance. Therefore, the volume and price of grain have broad and significant implications. In recent years, rice prices experienced a gradual rise in 1928 and 1929, followed by a severe decline in 1930 and 1931. As the time approaches when the old harvest is depleted and the new crop is not yet ready, the storage of rice remains a burden. If this year's grain growth is favorable, future grain prices will likely remain depressed. Farmers' sole product, grain, is already worth little, and other commodities such as silk and hemp are also struggling. Moreover, everyday necessities that were once made and supplied locally are now being displaced by new industries and imported from urban areas to rural villages. This is why the rural economy is bankrupt and farmers' lives are becoming increasingly difficult.

Shanghai is a major metropolis that relies on external sources for food, but it is also a hub for domestic and foreign grain transportation. Its market's prosperity or decline and price fluctuations affect both domestic and foreign markets. This article, based on grain price statistics from the Municipal Social Bureau, examines these changes to illustrate the hardship faced by farmers and merchants and the distress of the people due to the wild fluctuations in grain prices over the past five years.

In 1928, grain prices remained relatively stable, with no significant impact on farmers or consumers. The average annual price of japonica rice (calculated per picul, the same applies below) was 11.17 yuan, with a maximum of 12.02 yuan and a minimum of 9.91 yuan. The average price of indica rice was 9.32 yuan, with a maximum of 10.09 yuan and a minimum of 7.98 yuan. In 1929, the average annual price of japonica rice rose to 13.51 yuan. October saw the highest price at 16.28 yuan, while April saw the lowest at only 11.34 yuan. The average price of indica rice was 12.55 yuan, with a maximum of 14.90 yuan and a minimum of 10.89 yuan. Compared to 1928, the difference between the highest and lowest prices for japonica rice was 6.37 yuan per picul, and for indica rice, it was nearly 7 yuan. This was due to a sudden disaster affecting the rice crop in 1929, leading to a price surge when the new crop was released, an unprecedented occurrence. In the spring of 1930, influenced by the poor harvest of 1929, rice prices continued to rise, increasing by 1 to 2 yuan per picul in January and February compared to the end of 1929. Since the summer, various provinces experienced continuous rain disasters, leading to extreme panic in the grain market. Despite the import of large quantities of foreign rice, prices could not be suppressed. The soaring rice prices in the interior of Jiangsu and Zhejiang caused widespread anxiety both domestically and abroad. In June of that year, the average price of japonica rice in Shanghai reached 20.05 yuan, with actual prices exceeding 24 or 25 yuan per picul, nearly double the average price in September 1928. Prices remained around 19 yuan in July, August, and September. After October, with a bountiful autumn harvest, rice prices suddenly dropped. The average price in December was only 11.84 yuan, a decrease of more than half in six months. Such wild fluctuations were rare. Farmers had to use two piculs of new rice to compensate for the price of one picul during the lean season. This loss alone was severe. The average price of indica rice also reached its highest point in June of that year, at 18.72 yuan. After September, prices fell from 13.06 yuan to 11.32 yuan due to the abundant new crop and the influx of foreign rice. The difference between the highest and lowest prices exceeded 7 yuan. From January to July 1931, rice prices were very low due to the abundant autumn harvest of 1930. The average monthly price of japonica rice fluctuated around 12 to 13 yuan, with a minimum of only 10.63 yuan in April. The average price of indica rice, however, experienced an abnormal reversal, with prices in February, March, and April being higher than those of japonica rice, likely due to temporary supply and demand factors. After August, prices gradually returned to normal, but widespread flooding in rice-producing provinces along the Yangtze River caused panic, and rice prices surged again. The average price of japonica rice increased sharply to 16.3 yuan, while indica rice reached 14.58 yuan. Compared to July, prices rose by more than 3 yuan per picul in one month. After October, a significant change occurred. Foreign rice-producing countries such as Annam and Siam had abundant harvests and aggressively dumped their surplus grain abroad. Domestically, in the aftermath of the September 18th Incident, tensions with Japan escalated. Japanese merchants, who had taken advantage of the severe floods in China to stockpile large quantities of grain in Southeast Asian rice-producing countries, suddenly devalued and sold off their holdings. Subsequently, the domestic autumn harvest, although affected by floods, was not as severely impacted as anticipated, leading to a drastic drop in rice prices. Consequently, the average price of japonica rice in October was only 14.07 yuan, while indica rice was 12.65 yuan. In 1932, the January 28th Shanghai Incident suddenly erupted and lasted for four months. Although statistical data was interrupted, rice prices at the time remained stable due to mutual support and regulation among the public. In May and June, during the lean season, the average price of japonica rice was still around 14 yuan. After July, with sufficient domestic grain reserves and a flood of foreign rice in the market, prices continued to fall, creating the lowest prices in nearly a decade. The price in July was 13.90 yuan, in August it was 13.70 yuan, in September it was 12.32 yuan, in October it was 10.92 yuan, in November it was 9.69 yuan, and in December it was 9.10 yuan, a difference of twice the peak price in June 1930. The decline in the price of indica rice was similar to that of japonica rice. The average prices in May and June were 12.83 yuan and 12.99 yuan, respectively. After July, prices also consistently declined, falling from 11.70 yuan to 8.57 yuan. Since the spring of this year (1933), grain prices have remained low, and with the change to the market picul (which is smaller than the original sea picul), market prices appear even lower. A chart of the daily average prices of japonica and indica rice over the past five years is provided above for reference. (The chart is omitted).

In recent years, China's food production has been affected by frequent national disasters, including floods, droughts, and pests. Provinces such as Hunan, Hubei, Anhui, and Jiangxi, which were originally the most productive rice-growing areas in Central China, have been unable to maintain stable agricultural production due to the use of military force by communists, sometimes even requiring assistance from foreign rice imports. Even in Shanghai, where foreign rice imports were historically minimal, 1930 saw more than 7 million dan (a unit of weight) of foreign rice imported due to shortages in Jiangsu and Zhejiang. However, in 1932, foreign rice imports reached an unprecedented 22.48 million dan, valued at over 11.9 million gold units. Only 3.97 million dan were imported into Shanghai that year, suggesting that the majority was shipped to Guangdong and Fujian. Why did Guangdong and Fujian require such large quantities of foreign rice when rice-producing areas along the Yangtze River reported low grain prices? There are two possible reasons: (1) After hearing about the severe floods in the Yangtze River region in 1931, Guangdong and Fujian were concerned about insufficient domestic rice supplies and ordered large quantities of foreign rice in advance. (2) Due to banditry and the fear of disruption to coastal areas, Guangdong and Fujian, being coastal and grain-deficient provinces, needed to stockpile rice in case of a blockade, to prevent starvation and social unrest. In essence, the global economic downturn, which reached its peak last year, resulted in low prices. Asian rice-producing countries had abundant harvests, and the Republic of China, with its large population and lack of trade barriers, became the only destination for foreign rice. This is why our rice prices are so low, and foreign rice continues to be imported in large quantities despite the low prices. This is a cause for deep reflection.

Recent International Wheat Trends

— Zhide

Since the decline of agricultural products in our country, imports of rice and wheat have become increasingly important. However, with a relatively good autumn harvest last year, there was no need to order white rice. The development of mechanized flour milling industry and the lack of domestic wheat supply, covering only four to five months of demand, necessitates the import of foreign wheat. From August-September of last year to March-April of this year, the amount of wheat ordered totaled about 1.2 to 1.3 million tons. Recently, the overseas wheat market has undergone drastic changes, which are closely related to our country's flour and wheat industry. To clarify the situation for those within and outside the industry, this report, based on the information provided by the Hezhong Company, the actual sales of manufacturers, and recent trends, is presented below. Although the information is somewhat outdated, it is still a matter of international trade that cannot be ignored.

In the first half of March, due to the increasingly tense situation in North China, there was little development in terms of orders. However, the situation became slightly more active in the second half of the month, with approximately 60,000 tons of wheat being traded, including 40,000 tons of Argentinian wheat, 8,000 tons of Australian wheat, 4,500 tons of Canadian wheat, and 7,500 tons of Silesian wheat. Because import duties were added on foreign flour, North China proactively purchased Canadian and Australian flour in preparation. However, in April, the situation in North and South China changed. Due to disappointing news about the US wheat harvest and Roosevelt's policy of maintaining silver prices, speculators worldwide became enthusiastic, causing prices in Chicago and Canada to increase daily. However, there was no rush among Chinese merchants to place orders, with a total of only 41,000 tons being ordered, including 33,500 tons of Argentinian wheat for shipment to Shanghai and 4,000 tons of Argentinian wheat and 4,500 tons of Australian wheat for shipment to Qingdao. This was because previous orders from six months earlier were arriving, and the new domestic wheat crop was about to be harvested, so there was no need to place new orders. In May, the prices of foreign wheat continued to rise. Shanghai manufacturers were unwilling to sell at high prices amidst the domestic economic decline, and they still had approximately two to three months' worth of wheat from previous orders. Therefore, although foreign firms were selling, manufacturers were not interested. The trends in the world's major wheat markets are summarized below.

Canadian wheat prices experienced significant fluctuations. Due to exchange rate issues, it often could not be imported into China. However, it was very popular in the US market. In late March, there were a few transactions in Shanghai because the exchange rate favored imports, but afterwards, there was no further activity because it was still restricted by the exchange rate. However, in terms of the wheat that had already arrived, its quality was high, and manufacturers mixed it into their production (which was very popular among users). The price fluctuations on the Winnipeg Grain Exchange for March, April, and May are listed below (in Canadian dollars per gold unit):

The development of Australian wheat has been quite noteworthy recently. Approximately one million tons have been ordered by our country from last year to now. However, farmers there are only interested in selling their surplus. Recently, everything has been sold out. Not only is no one selling, but even if there were, it would only be at the highest price. Moreover, the local market is purely independent and has little connection to the price fluctuations in the US and Canadian markets. As for its quality, based on what Shanghai manufacturers have received, it has thin skins and white color, so the flour yield is better than other types of wheat. Its weight is between 62 and 63 pounds. However, South Australian wheat is of lower quality, with a standard of only 60 pounds. The quality of Australian wheat over the past three years, measured by weight per bushel, is listed below:

When Chicago wheat prices were around 46 cents, North American wheat had a good chance of competing in China and the world. However, with the recent price increases, such a scenario is unlikely. The main reasons for the price increases are the anticipated poor harvest this winter, the reduction in yields, and the complex interplay of political changes and economic problems, all of which are driving up wheat prices. Argentine wheat also plays an important role in the world. Its annual production is approximately 6.3 million tons, with exports of approximately 4 million tons. Chinese manufacturers have paid little attention to it, but this year, it has been particularly cheap, about 10-20% cheaper than Australian and Canadian wheat at the time. Its standard weight is between 60 and 63.5 pounds. However, this Argentine wheat has recently arrived in Shanghai, and manufacturers have found that its grains are finer and lighter, significantly different from Canadian wheat. Therefore, the high volume of this wheat may not be suitable for Chinese manufacturers. The first ship of Argentine wheat from Bahia Blanca in 1932-1933 took 49 days at sea, and its natural weight was 75.2 kilograms per hectoliter, approximately 60.3 pounds per bushel.

Based on the above trends in world wheat production and the state of domestic wheat production in our country, Chinese flour mills cannot maintain production without foreign wheat supplies. China, which has been known as an agricultural country for thousands of years, and is a country of vast land and abundant resources, now relies on the world for its primary food supply. How will those in power remedy this situation?
